Since A is on Ω, the quadratic in t (|A + t*(P - A) - M|^2 - r1^2 = 0) will have one root t=0, the other root t_E gives E. So we can compute t_E by Vieta, using that the coefficient of t^2 and t. Similarly for F. That's a common technique.

So define direction vector v = P - A. Then param: X(t) = A + t v. Then we need |X(t) - M|^2 = r1^2. Since M = (0,0). So |A + t v|^2 = r1^2. We know |A|^2 = r1^2. So equation:

|A + t v|^2 = r1^2 → (A + t v)·(A + t v) = r1^2 → |A|^2 + 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = r1^2. Since |A|^2 = r1^2, we have:

2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 A·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So roots t=0 and t = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Thus the second intersection is at t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Similarly for Γ with center N and radius r2: we solve |A + t v - N|^2 = r2^2. Since A is on Γ, we have |A - N|^2 = r2^2. Then similar derivation: let w = A - N. Then equation: |(A - N) + t v|^2 = r2^2 → |A - N|^2 + 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = r2^2. Cancel |A - N|^2 = r2^2 → 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 (A - N)·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So t_F = -2 ((A - N)·v) / |v|^2.

Thus we can get coordinates of E and F explicitly in terms of A, P, M, N. Then we need circumcircle of triangle BEF. B is symmetric of A across x-axis. So B = (x_A, -y_A). E and F are some points. Compute circumcircle of BEF. Use coordinates:

Find circle. I'll compute using formula. Let’s denote O_ = (Ox, Oy). We'll solve |O_-B|^2 = |O_-E|^2 = |O_-F|^2. Subtract equations:

|O_-B|^2 - |O_-E|^2 = 0 → (Ox^2 + (Oy+3)^2) - ( (Ox-2.4)^2 + (Oy+1.8)^2) = 0 → expand: Ox^2 + (Oy^2+6Oy+9) - (Ox^2 -4.8Ox +5.76 + Oy^2 +3.6Oy +3.24) = 0 → cancel Ox^2, Oy^2: 6Oy+9 - (-4.8Ox +5.76 +3.6Oy +3.24) = 6Oy+9 +4.8Ox -5.76 -3.6Oy -3.24 = 4.8Ox + (6Oy-3.6Oy) + (9-5.76-3.24) = 4.8Ox + 2.4Oy + (9-9) = 4.8Ox + 2.4Oy = 0 → divide 2.4: 2Ox + Oy = 0 → Oy = -2Ox.

Similarly, |O_-B|^2 - |O_-F|^2 = 0 → (Ox^2 + (Oy+3)^2) - ( (Ox-4)^2 + (Oy+5)^2 ) = 0 → expand: Ox^2 + (Oy^2+6Oy+9) - (Ox^2 -8Ox +16 + Oy^2 +10Oy +25) = 0 → cancel: 6Oy+9 - (-8Ox +16 +10Oy +25) = 6Oy+9 +8Ox -16 -10Oy -25 = 8Ox + (6Oy-10Oy) + (9-16-25) = 8Ox -4Oy -32 = 0 → divide 4: 2Ox - Oy -8 = 0 → 2Ox - Oy = 8. Substitute Oy = -2Ox → 2Ox - (-2Ox) = 4Ox = 8 → Ox = 2, Oy = -4. So center O_ = (2, -4). Then radius squared = distance to B: (2-0)^2 + (-4+3)^2 = 4 + 1 = 5. So radius = sqrt(5) ≈ 2.236. Now line L: through H(3,-1) direction (1,-2). Equation param: (3,-1)+t(1,-2). Compute distance from O_ (2,-4) to this line. The direction vector d = (1,-2). The line passes through H. The distance from O_ to line L is |(O_ - H) × d| / |d| (in 2D, cross product magnitude). Compute O_ - H = (2-3, -4+1) = (-1, -3). Cross product (2D) with d = (1,-2): magnitude = |(-1)*(-2) - (-3)*1| = |2 - (-3)| = |5| = 5. |d| = sqrt(1+4)= sqrt5. So distance = 5 / sqrt5 = sqrt5. That equals radius. So indeed L is tangent to the circumcircle. So the statement holds in this numeric example.

The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. Slope PN = (p_y - 0) / (p_x - d) = p_y / (p_x - d). So altitude from M has slope = -(p_x - d) / p_y (provided p_y ≠ 0). Similarly, altitude from N is perpendicular to PM. Slope PM = (p_y - 0) / (p_x - 0) = p_y / p_x. So altitude from N has slope = -p_x / p_y (provided p_y ≠ 0). Since P is not on MN (p_y ≠ 0 because A is not on MN and P is not on MN generally). In our numeric example, p_y = -3, so fine. So we can compute H as intersection of lines:

Line L_M (altitude from M): passes through M (0,0) with slope m1 = - (p_x - d) / p_y.
Line L_N (altitude from N): passes through N (d,0) with slope m2 = - p_x / p_y.

Then H = intersection. That yields H coordinates. That seems manageable. Then we need the line through H parallel to AP. So we need direction vector v = P - A. That's fine.

Now compute E and F using t_E, t_F. Then compute circumcircle of BEF. We need to show that distance from its center to line L (through H parallel to AP) equals its radius. Let's denote:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)

Let r1, r2 > 0, r1 < r2, with |r2 - r1| < d < r1 + r2.

Define:

a = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). (x-coordinate of A)
h = sqrt(r1^2 - a^2). (positive)

Thus A = (a, h), B = (a, -h).

C = (-r1, 0)
D = (d + r2, 0)

Now P = (p_x, p_y). We have:

p_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

We also derived p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h.

But we should also check if P is uniquely defined; yes, the perpendicular bisector of CD is x = p_x, and the perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD) will intersect at a point; that point is P. However our expression for p_y derived from |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2 is valid, but we need to ensure that this P also satisfies |P-D|^2 = |P-C|^2 automatically because x_P is midpoint of CD. That's true. So P is determined.

Now we need to compute v = P - A = (p_x - a, p_y - h). That will be messy but maybe simplifies.

Let's compute p_x - a and p_y - h in terms of r1, r2, d.

First compute a:

a = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d).

p_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

So p_x - a = (d + r2 - r1)/2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = 1/2 * [ (d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/d ].

Simplify: (d + r2 - r1) = (d^2 + d(r2 - r1))/d? Actually combine over denominator d:

p_x - a = 1/2 * [ (d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/d ] = 1/2 * [ (d(d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)) / d ].

Compute numerator:

d(d + r2 - r1) = d^2 + d(r2 - r1). Then subtract (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) → we get:

d^2 + d(r2 - r1) - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2 = d(r2 - r1) - r1^2 + r2^2.

So p_x - a = 1/(2d) * [ d(r2 - r1) - r1^2 + r2^2 ].

Simplify - r1^2 + r2^2 = (r2^2 - r1^2) = (r2 - r1)(r2 + r1). So:

p_x - a = 1/(2d) * [ d(r2 - r1) + (r2 - r1)(r2 + r1) ] = 1/(2d) * (r2 - r1) ( d + r2 + r1 ).

Thus p_x - a = (r2 - r1)(d + r1 + r2) / (2d).

That's nice. So v_x = (r2 - r1)(d + r1 + r2) / (2d). Since r2 > r1, positive.

Now compute p_y. p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h. We need to simplify r1 + a. We have a expressed. Let's compute r1 + a:

r1 + a = r1 + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = (2d r1 + d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2d r1 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So r1 + a = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). That's positive.

Now h = sqrt(r1^2 - a^2). We can also compute h^2 = r1^2 - a^2. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for p_y in terms of known quantities, maybe it's something like - (r2^2 - r1^2 - d^2) / (2h) or something. Let's compute p_y explicitly:

p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h.

Plug p_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2. So p_y = - ( (d + r2 - r1)/2 * (r1 + a) ) / h = - (d + r2 - r1)(r1 + a) / (2h).

Now r1 + a = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So product:

(d + r2 - r1) * ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Then p_y = - [ (d + r2 - r1) * ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) ] / (2d * 2h) = - (d + r2 - r1) * ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (4 d h).

That seems messy. But perhaps we can find an expression for p_y that is simpler by using the relation from the perpendicular bisector of AC. Maybe we can compute P's coordinates directly by solving for P as the circumcenter of ACD. Another way: Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical, and the perpendicular bisector of AC is something. The intersection yields P. So we could compute P's y-coordinate by solving the equation that P lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC: (P - (A+C)/2) · (C - A) = 0. That gives a linear equation in P (since (C-A) is known). Combined with x-coordinate constant, we can solve for y. That might be simpler than using the distance equality. Let's try that.

Let A = (a, h), C = (-r1, 0). The midpoint of AC: ((a - r1)/2, h/2). The vector AC = ( -r1 - a, 0 - h) = ( -(a + r1), -h). The perpendicular bisector condition: (P - midpoint) · AC = 0. So (x_P - (a - r1)/2, y_P - h/2) · ( -(a + r1), -h) = 0. That gives:

-(a + r1) (x_P - (a - r1)/2) - h (y_P - h/2) = 0.

We know x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2. So we can solve for y_P. That yields a linear equation. Let's do that.

Compute:

-(a + r1) (x_P - (a - r1)/2) - h (y_P - h/2) = 0.

Simplify:

-(a + r1) (x_P - (a - r1)/2) = h (y_P - h/2). Then we can solve for y_P.

But maybe we can compute p_y more directly: Since P is circumcenter, we have that P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of AC is a line. We could compute its equation and then intersect with x = p_x. That's a linear equation. That seems easier than the earlier distance equality. But the distance equality gave an expression involving p_x (r1 + a) / h, which seems already derived. However we need to ensure that it's correct. Let's verify with the numeric example: r1=3, r2=5, d=4, a=0, h=3, p_x=3, r1+a=3, then p_y = - (3*3)/3 = -3. That matches. So the formula p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h is correct. But we derived it from |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2, which gave - x_P x_A - y_P y_A = r1 x_P. Then we used |A|^2 = r1^2 and canceled. Let's double-check that derivation thoroughly.

We have |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. Write:

(P - A)·(P - A) = (P - C)·(P - C). Expand: |P|^2 - 2 P·A + |A|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2. Cancel |P|^2:

-2 P·A + |A|^2 = -2 P·C + |C|^2 → bring terms: -2 P·A + 2 P·C = |C|^2 - |A|^2 → 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

So we have 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

That is a linear equation in P. That's simpler! Because earlier I used equality of squares but got a different expression. Let's re-evaluate: I had (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2. Then I expanded and canceled x_P^2, y_P^2, obtaining -2 x_P x_A + x_A^2 - 2 y_P y_A + y_A^2 = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2. That seems correct. But the alternative form 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2 is derived from |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. Let's verify:

|P - A|^2 - |P - C|^2 = 0 → (|P|^2 - 2 P·A + |A|^2) - (|P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2) = -2 P·A + |A|^2 + 2 P·C - |C|^2 = 2 P·(C - A) + (|A|^2 - |C|^2) = 0 → 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2. Yes. So that's correct. So the linear equation is:

2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

Now we also have that P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD: |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2 gives a linear equation too, which gave x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2. That is consistent. So we can solve for P using two linear equations. That might be easier.

We have P = (p_x, p_y). C = (-r1, 0). A = (a, h). Then C - A = (-r1 - a, -h). Then P·(C - A) = p_x (-r1 - a) + p_y (-h) = - (r1 + a) p_x - h p_y. Then 2 * that = |C|^2 - |A|^2. |C|^2 = r1^2. |A|^2 = r1^2. So |C|^2 - |A|^2 = 0. Therefore we get:

2 [ - (r1 + a) p_x - h p_y ] = 0 → - (r1 + a) p_x - h p_y = 0 → (r1 + a) p_x + h p_y = 0. That's interesting! Because |C|^2 = r1^2, |A|^2 = r1^2, so indeed the right-hand side is zero. So we get:

But we know A·P = - r1 p. Also N·P = d p + 0 * q = d p. So N·P = d p. And N·A = d a + 0 * h = d a.

Thus (A - N)·v = (- r1 p) - (d p) - r1^2 + d a = -p (r1 + d) - r1^2 + d a.

Now a expressed as (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So d a = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2. So

(A - N)·v = -p (r1 + d) - r1^2 + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2.

Simplify: - r1^2 + (r1^2)/2 = -r1^2/2. So

(A - N)·v = -p (r1 + d) - (r1^2)/2 + (d^2 - r2^2)/2.

But maybe we can also express p in terms of d, r1, r2. p = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

Then p (r1 + d) = (d + r2 - r1)/2 * (d + r1) = ( (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1) )/2. Let's compute that:

(d + r2 - r1)(d + r1) = (d + r2 - r1)*(d + r1) = (d + (r2 - r1))*(d + r1). Expand: (d)*(d + r1) + (r2 - r1)*(d + r1) = d^2 + d r1 + (r2 - r1)d + (r2 - r1) r1 = d^2 + d r1 + d r2 - d r1 + (r2^2 - r1^2) = d^2 + d r2 + (r2^2 - r1^2). So indeed = d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2. That's a symmetric expression.

Thus p (r1 + d) = (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2)/2.

Then (A - N)·v = - (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2)/2 - r1^2/2 + (d^2 - r2^2)/2.

Combine: Combine the fractions:

- (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2)/2 - (r1^2)/2 + (d^2 - r2^2)/2.

Combine numerators over denominator 2:

= [ - (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2) - r1^2 + (d^2 - r2^2) ] / 2.

Simplify inside:

- d^2 - d r2 - r2^2 + r1^2 - r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2 = (-d^2 + d^2) + (-d r2) + (-r2^2 - r2^2) + (r1^2 - r1^2) = - d r2 - 2 r2^2.

Thus (A - N)·v = ( - d r2 - 2 r2^2 ) / 2 = - (d r2 + 2 r2^2)/2 = - r2 (d + 2 r2)/2.

That's interesting. So t_F = -2 * ( (A - N)·v ) / |v|^2 = -2 * ( - r2 (d + 2 r2)/2 ) / |v|^2 = ( r2 (d + 2 r2) ) / |v|^2.

But check: (A - N)·v = - r2 (d + 2 r2)/2. So -2 times that is r2 (d + 2 r2). So t_F = r2 (d + 2 r2) / |v|^2. However earlier we derived t_F = -2 ((A - N)·v) / |v|^2. That's correct. So t_F = r2 (d + 2 r2) / |v|^2. But we should double-check the sign and whether this yields F on Γ with A and P collinear. In numeric example, r2=5, d=4, then r2(d+2r2)=5*(4+10)=5*14=70. |v|^2=45, so t_F = 70/45 = 14/9 ≈ 1.555... But earlier we computed t_F = 4/3 ≈ 1.333. There's a discrepancy. Given the complexity, perhaps there is a synthetic solution that is more elegant. Let's try to think synthetically.

We have two circles Ω (center M) and Γ (center N). They intersect at A and B. The line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with C, M, N, D in that order. So C is on Ω on the ray opposite to N from M; D is on Γ on the ray opposite to M from N. So M is between C and N, N is between M and D.

Let P be the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN. The circumcenter P is somewhere on that line. Also, P is equidistant from A and C. So P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC.

Now consider line AP. It intersects Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So A, E, P are collinear; A, F, P are collinear; thus E, F, A, P are collinear. So EF is a segment of line AP. So line AP is a common secant of the two circles (through A). Since A is a common point, AP is a line through A, so it's a secant of both circles.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N lie on the line MN, and P is not on MN, triangle PMN has base MN. The orthocenter H can be constructed as follows: The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN; the altitude from N is perpendicular to PM. Their intersection is H. That's fine.

We need to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Let's denote L = line through H parallel to AP.

Observation: Since AP is a line through A, and B is symmetric across MN, maybe the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle with diameter something after a homothety centered at H? Or perhaps we can prove that the reflection of H across the line through O (center of (BEF)) perpendicular to L lies on the circle.

Another approach: Use coordinate geometry but choose a coordinate system that simplifies the expressions. We already set d=1. Let's compute all quantities in terms of r1 and r2. I'll try to compute |v|^2 = AP^2 = R^2, but perhaps we can compute R^2 in a nice form. Since we have R = (AC * AD) / (2 h). So R^2 = (AC^2 * AD^2) / (4 h^2). So we need AC^2 * AD^2 / h^2. That might simplify to something like ( (1 + r1 + r2)^2 * (r1 r2) ) / (4 something). Let's compute.

First, compute AC^2 = r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2). We can also compute AC^2 in terms of r1 and a. But maybe it's better to compute AC^2 and AD^2 in terms of a and h, then h^2. But we also have a and h. Let's compute h^2 using the common chord formula. That is:

h^2 = (1/16) * ( ( (r1 + r2)^2 - 1 ) ( 1 - (r2 - r1)^2 ) ). That's symmetric.

Now AC^2 = (a + r1)^2 + h^2. But we have an expression: AC^2 = 2 r1 (r1 + a). And r1 + a = ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2)/2. So AC^2 = 2 r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2)/2 = r1 ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2). So AC^2 = r1 ( (r1+1)^2 - r2^2). That's a cubic.

Similarly, AD^2 = (a - (1+r2))^2 + h^2. But we can also express AD^2 in a symmetric way using the formula for chord length in Γ. Since A and D are on Γ, and N is center. Let’s compute AD^2 = 2 r2^2 - 2 r2 (a-1). That's one expression. Then we can express a-1 in terms of r1,r2. a-1 = (r1^2 - r2^2 - 1)/2. So AD^2 = 2 r2^2 - 2 r2 * ((r1^2 - r2^2 - 1)/2) = 2 r2^2 - r2 (r1^2 - r2^2 - 1) = 2 r2^2 - r1^2 r2 + r2^3 + r2. So AD^2 = r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2. That's also a cubic.

Now h^2 is expressed as (1/16) * ( ( (r1+r2)^2 - 1 ) ( 1 - (r2 - r1)^2 ) ). Expand: ((r1+r2)^2 - 1) = (r1^2 + 2 r1 r2 + r2^2 - 1). (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) = 1 - (r2^2 - 2 r1 r2 + r1^2) = 1 - r1^2 - r2^2 + 2 r1 r2. So product = (r1^2 + 2 r1 r2 + r2^2 - 1) * (1 - r1^2 - r2^2 + 2 r1 r2). That's symmetric. So

h^2 = (1/16) * ( (r1^2 + r2^2 + 2 r1 r2 - 1) * (1 - r1^2 - r2^2 + 2 r1 r2) ). Let’s denote S = r1^2 + r2^2 + 2 r1 r2 - 1 = (r1 + r2)^2 - 1. And T = 1 - (r1^2 + r2^2) + 2 r1 r2 = 1 - (r1^2 + r2^2) + 2 r1 r2 = 1 - (r1^2 - 2 r1 r2 + r2^2) = 1 - (r1 - r2)^2. So indeed h^2 = (1/16) * S * T. That's a nice expression.

Now compute AC^2 * AD^2. AC^2 = r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2) = r1 * ( (r1^2 + 2 r1 + 1) - r2^2 ) = r1 * (r1^2 + 2 r1 + 1 - r2^2). So AC^2 = r1^3 + 2 r1^2 + r1 - r1 r2^2.

AD^2 = r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2.

So product is (r1^3 + 2 r1^2 + r1 - r1 r2^2) * (r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2). That looks like a symmetric expression if we interchange r1 and r2, but note that the terms are not symmetric due to the - r1 r2^2 and - r1^2 r2. But overall, the product should be symmetric? Because AC^2 and AD^2 are not symmetric individually, but the product might be symmetric after multiplication. Let's check: If we swap r1 and r2, AC^2 becomes r2 * ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2) = r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2, which is exactly AD^2. So AD^2 is the swapped version of AC^2. So the product AC^2 * AD^2 is symmetric under swapping r1 and r2. That's good.

Now R^2 = (AC^2 * AD^2) / (4 h^2). So we can compute R^2 in terms of r1,r2. That might simplify.

Let's compute AC^2 * AD^2. Write AC^2 = r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2). Similarly, AD^2 = r2 * ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2). So product = r1 r2 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2) * ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2). That's a product. Let's compute each factor.

((r1+1)^2 - r2^2) = (r1+1 - r2)(r1+1 + r2) = (r1 - r2 + 1)(r1 + r2 + 1). Similarly, ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2) = (r2 - r1 + 1)(r2 + r1 + 1). Actually (r1 - r2 + 1) and (r2 - r1 + 1) are different unless r1=r2. Their product is ( (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r2 - r1 + 1) = ( (1 + (r1 - r2)) * (1 - (r1 - r2)) ) = (1 - (r1 - r2)^2) = (1 - (r2 - r1)^2). That's the T factor in h^2. And (r1 + r2 + 1) appears in both factors. So we have:

AC^2 = r1 * (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r1 + r2 + 1).
AD^2 = r2 * (r2 - r1 + 1) * (r1 + r2 + 1).

Thus AC^2 * AD^2 = r1 r2 * (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r2 - r1 + 1) * (r1 + r2 + 1)^2.

Now (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r2 - r1 + 1) = (1 + (r1 - r2)) * (1 - (r1 - r2)) = 1 - (r1 - r2)^2 = 1 - (r2 - r1)^2. That's exactly the factor T in h^2 (up to a constant). So

AC^2 * AD^2 = r1 r2 * (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) * (r1 + r2 + 1)^2.

R^2 = (AC^2 * AD^2) / (4 h^2) = [ r1 r2 * (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) * (r1 + r2 + 1)^2 ] / (4 h^2).

We have:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → (O - B)·(O - B) = (O - E)·(O - E) → -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 O·E + |E|^2 → 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. (Eq1)

Similarly, 2 O·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2. (Eq2)

Now, we have ω: circle centered at P radius p. The points A, C, D are on ω. Since M is on ℓ, and C is on ℓ, we can consider the coordinates. This seems more tractable.

We can attempt to compute everything in this coordinate system. Let's define:

A = (0,0). Let P = (p,0) with p > 0. Then ω: (x-p)^2 + y^2 = p^2 → x^2 - 2p x + p^2 + y^2 = p^2 → x^2 + y^2 - 2p x = 0 → (x - p)^2 + y^2 = p^2. So ω passes through A.

Now, ℓ is a line that intersects ω at C and D. Also, ℓ contains M and N. Since M and N are centers of circles, and A is on both circles, we have that M and N are on ℓ, and also |M| = r1, |N| = r2. Also, C and D are on ℓ. The order is C-M-N-D along ℓ. So ℓ is a secant line of ω. The points C and D are the two intersections of ℓ with ω. So ℓ is a line that cuts ω at C and D. Since A is also on ω, but A is not on ℓ generally (unless ℓ passes through A). So ℓ is a secant line that does not pass through A. So the chord CD of ω is along ℓ.

Now, we have the circles Ω (M) and Γ (N). They intersect at A and B. Since A is on ω, but also on Ω and Γ. The line ℓ is the line of centers. The common chord AB is perpendicular to ℓ. So AB ⟂ ℓ. Also, the midpoint of AB lies on ℓ. That is a property.

Let θ be the angle between ℓ and the positive x-axis (AP). Since AP is horizontal, ℓ has some slope. Let’s define the direction vector of ℓ as v = (cos θ, sin θ). Then ℓ is a line with direction v. So the t values for C and D are the solutions to |h n + t v - P|^2 = p^2. That's a quadratic. The two solutions are t_C and t_D. The midpoint of CD is at t = (t_C + t_D)/2. That point is on ℓ. Also, note that the line ℓ is a secant, so the chord CD of ω is along ℓ. The center of ω is P = (p,0). So the perpendicular from P to ℓ is the line that passes through the midpoint of CD. So the midpoint of CD is the projection of P onto ℓ. That's a known fact: For a circle, the line from the center to a chord is perpendicular to the chord and passes through its midpoint. So the midpoint of CD is the foot of perpendicular from P to ℓ. So if we denote Q = projection of P onto ℓ, then Q is the midpoint of CD. So Q lies on ℓ. Also, Q is the point on ℓ that minimizes distance to P. So Q = h n + t_Q v for some t_Q. And we have |Q - P| is the distance from center to chord, which is sqrt(p^2 - (CD/2)^2). That's fine.

Now, M and N are also on ℓ. So we can think of ℓ as a line with coordinate t. Then C and D correspond to t_C and t_D. M and N correspond to t_M and t_N. The order C-M-N-D implies t_C < t_M < t_N < t_D. So M and N are between C and D.

|M| = r1. (1)

The quadratic in x: (x - m_x)^2 + m_y^2 = r1^2 → x^2 - 2 m_x x + m_x^2 + m_y^2 = r1^2 → x^2 - 2 m_x x + r1^2 = r1^2 → x^2 - 2 m_x x = 0 → x(x - 2 m_x) = 0. So the solutions are x = 0 and x = 2 m_x. But we could also compute using the fact that in a triangle, the orthocenter is the sum of the vertices' position vectors relative to the circumcenter. Actually H = A + B + C - 2 O (if O is circumcenter). That's a vector identity in Euclidean geometry when we use coordinates with origin at arbitrary point, but we need to be careful: For triangle with vertices A,B,C, let O be circumcenter. Then we have \(\vec{OH} = \vec{OA} + \vec{OB} + \vec{OC}\). In terms of position vectors relative to an arbitrary origin, if we denote vectors a,b,c, and o, then h = a + b + c - 2 o. Yes, that's correct because \(\vec{OH} = \vec{OA} + \vec{OB} + \vec{OC}\) implies \(h - o = (a - o) + (b - o) + (c - o) = a + b + c - 3o\). Then h = a + b + c - 2o. So indeed H = A + B + C - 2 O. So we can compute H if we know the circumcenter O of triangle PMN. That might be simpler because we can compute O from coordinates. But we also need O of PMN. Let O = (u_O, w_O) with w_O = (t_M + t_N)/2. Then we need |O - P|^2 = |O - M|^2.

Compute:

|O - P|^2 = (u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2.
|O - M|^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.

Set equal:

(u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.

Left: u_O^2 - 2 u_O p_u + p_u^2 + w_O^2 - 2 w_O p_w + p_w^2.
Right: u_O^2 - 2 u_O h + h^2 + w_O^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2.

-2 u_O p_u + p_u^2 - 2 w_O p_w + p_w^2 = -2 u_O h + h^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2.

Expand and cancel w_O^2? Actually we can solve for u_O. Let's do it.

Set:

(u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.

Expand:

Cancel u_O^2, w_O^2:

Thus, (h^2 - p_u^2) + (t_M^2 - p_w^2) = r1^2 - p^2.

2 u_O (h - p_u) = (r1^2 - p^2) + 2 w_O (p_w - t_M).   (A')

Actually if we consider line through A intersecting circle at A and another point X, then the directed distances satisfy (A A) * (A X) = (power of A?) But A is on the circle, so power of A is 0. That doesn't give product. However we can param: A + t*(P - A). At t=0 we get A, at t=1 we get P. Then the second intersection E corresponds to some t_E ≠ 0 such that point lies on Ω. Since A is on Ω, the quadratic in t (|A + t*(P - A) - M|^2 - r1^2 = 0) will have one root t=0, the other root t_E gives E. So we can compute t_E by Vieta, using that the coefficient of t^2 and t. Similarly for F. That's a common technique.

So define direction vector v = P - A. Then param: X(t) = A + t v. Then we need |X(t) - M|^2 = r1^2. Since M = (0,0). So |A + t v|^2 = r1^2. We know |A|^2 = r1^2. So equation:

|A + t v|^2 = r1^2 → (A + t v)·(A + t v) = r1^2 → |A|^2 + 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = r1^2. Since |A|^2 = r1^2, we have:

2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 A·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So roots t=0 and t = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Thus the second intersection is at t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Similarly for Γ with center N and radius r2: we solve |A + t v - N|^2 = r2^2. Since A is on Γ, we have |A - N|^2 = r2^2. Then similar derivation: let w = A - N. Then equation: |(A - N) + t v|^2 = r2^2 → |A - N|^2 + 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = r2^2. Cancel |A - N|^2 = r2^2 → 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 (A - N)·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So t_F = -2 ((A - N)·v) / |v|^2.

Thus we can get coordinates of E and F explicitly in terms of A, P, M, N. Then we need circumcircle of triangle BEF. B is symmetric of A across x-axis. So B = (x_A, -y_A). E and F are some points. |O_-B|^2 - |O_-E|^2 = 0 → (Ox^2 + (Oy+3)^2) - ( (Ox-2.4)^2 + (Oy+1.8)^2) = 0 → expand: Ox^2 + (Oy^2+6Oy+9) - (Ox^2 -4.8Ox +5.76 + Oy^2 +3.6Oy +3.24) = 0 → cancel Ox^2, Oy^2: 6Oy+9 - (-4.8Ox +5.76 +3.6Oy +3.24) = 6Oy+9 +4.8Ox -5.76 -3.6Oy -3.24 = 4.8Ox + (6Oy-3.6Oy) + (9-5.76-3.24) = 4.8Ox + 2.4Oy + (9-9) = 4.8Ox + 2.4Oy = 0 → divide 2.4: 2Ox + Oy = 0 → Oy = -2Ox.

Similarly, |O_-B|^2 - |O_-F|^2 = 0 → (Ox^2 + (Oy+3)^2) - ( (Ox-4)^2 + (Oy+5)^2 ) = 0 → expand: Ox^2 + (Oy^2+6Oy+9) - (Ox^2 -8Ox +16 + Oy^2 +10Oy +25) = 0 → cancel: 6Oy+9 - (-8Ox +16 +10Oy +25) = 6Oy+9 +8Ox -16 -10Oy -25 = 8Ox + (6Oy-10Oy) + (9-16-25) = 8Ox -4Oy -32 = 0 → divide 4: 2Ox - Oy -8 = 0 → 2Ox - Oy = 8. Substitute Oy = -2Ox → 2Ox - (-2Ox) = 4Ox = 8 → Ox = 2, Oy = -4. So center O_ = (2, -4). Then radius squared = distance to B: (2-0)^2 + (-4+3)^2 = 4 + 1 = 5. So radius = sqrt(5) ≈ 2.236. Now line L: through H(3,-1) direction (1,-2). Equation param: (3,-1)+t(1,-2). Compute distance from O_ (2,-4) to this line. The direction vector d = (1,-2). The line passes through H. The distance from O_ to line L is |(O_ - H) × d| / |d| (in 2D, cross product magnitude). Compute O_ - H = (2-3, -4+1) = (-1, -3). Cross product (2D) with d = (1,-2): magnitude = |(-1)*(-2) - (-3)*1| = |2 - (-3)| = |5| = 5. |d| = sqrt(1+4)= sqrt5. So distance = 5 / sqrt5 = sqrt5. That equals radius. So indeed L is tangent to the circumcircle. So the statement holds in this numeric example.

Similarly, altitude from N is perpendicular to PM. Slope PM = (p_y - 0) / (p_x - 0) = p_y / p_x. So altitude from N has slope = -p_x / p_y (provided p_y ≠ 0). Since P is not on MN (p_y ≠ 0 because A is not on MN and P is not on MN generally). In our numeric example, p_y = -3, so fine. So we can compute H as intersection of lines:

Line L_M (altitude from M): passes through M (0,0) with slope m1 = - (p_x - d) / p_y.
Line L_N (altitude from N): passes through N (d,0) with slope m2 = - p_x / p_y.

Then H = intersection. That yields H coordinates. That seems manageable. Then we need the line through H parallel to AP. So we need direction vector v = P - A. That's fine.

Let's denote:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)

Let r1, r2 > 0, r1 < r2, with |r2 - r1| < d < r1 + r2.

Define:

a = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). (x-coordinate of A)
h = sqrt(r1^2 - a^2). (positive)

Thus A = (a, h), B = (a, -h).

C = (-r1, 0)
D = (d + r2, 0)

Now P = (p_x, p_y). We have:

p_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

We also derived p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h.

But we should also check if P is uniquely defined; yes, the perpendicular bisector of CD is x = p_x, and the perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD) will intersect at a point; that point is P. However our expression for p_y derived from |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2 is valid, but we need to ensure that this P also satisfies |P-D|^2 = |P-C|^2 automatically because x_P is midpoint of CD. That's true. So P is determined.

Now we need to compute v = P - A = (p_x - a, p_y - h). That will be messy but maybe simplifies.

Let's compute p_x - a and p_y - h in terms of r1, r2, d.

First compute a:

a = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d).

p_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

So p_x - a = (d + r2 - r1)/2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = 1/2 * [ (d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/d ].

Simplify: (d + r2 - r1) = (d^2 + d(r2 - r1))/d? Actually combine over denominator d:

p_x - a = 1/2 * [ (d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/d ] = 1/2 * [ (d(d + r2 - r1) - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)) / d ].

Compute numerator:

d(d + r2 - r1) = d^2 + d(r2 - r1). Then subtract (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) → we get:

d^2 + d(r2 - r1) - d^2 - r1^2 + r2^2 = d(r2 - r1) - r1^2 + r2^2.

So p_x - a = 1/(2d) * [ d(r2 - r1) - r1^2 + r2^2 ].

Simplify - r1^2 + r2^2 = (r2^2 - r1^2) = (r2 - r1)(r2 + r1). So:

p_x - a = 1/(2d) * [ d(r2 - r1) + (r2 - r1)(r2 + r1) ] = 1/(2d) * (r2 - r1) ( d + r2 + r1 ).

Thus p_x - a = (r2 - r1)(d + r1 + r2) / (2d).

That's nice. So v_x = (r2 - r1)(d + r1 + r2) / (2d). Since r2 > r1, positive.

Now compute p_y. p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h. We need to simplify r1 + a. We have a expressed. Let's compute r1 + a:

r1 + a = r1 + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = (2d r1 + d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2d r1 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d) = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So r1 + a = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). That's positive.

Now h = sqrt(r1^2 - a^2). We can also compute h^2 = r1^2 - a^2. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for p_y in terms of known quantities, maybe it's something like - (r2^2 - r1^2 - d^2) / (2h) or something. Let's compute p_y explicitly:

p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h.

Plug p_x = (d + r2 - r1)/2. So p_y = - ( (d + r2 - r1)/2 * (r1 + a) ) / h = - (d + r2 - r1)(r1 + a) / (2h).

Now r1 + a = ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So product:

(d + r2 - r1) * ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (2d). Then p_y = - [ (d + r2 - r1) * ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) ] / (2d * 2h) = - (d + r2 - r1) * ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) / (4 d h).

That seems messy. But perhaps we can find an expression for p_y that is simpler by using the relation from the perpendicular bisector of AC. Maybe we can compute P's coordinates directly by solving for P as the circumcenter of ACD. Another way: Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical, and the perpendicular bisector of AC is something. The intersection yields P. So we could compute P's y-coordinate by solving the equation that P lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC: (P - (A+C)/2) · (C - A) = 0. That gives a linear equation in P (since (C-A) is known). Combined with x-coordinate constant, we can solve for y. That might be simpler than using the distance equality. Let's try that.

Let A = (a, h), C = (-r1, 0). The midpoint of AC: ((a - r1)/2, h/2). The vector AC = ( -r1 - a, 0 - h) = ( -(a + r1), -h). The perpendicular bisector condition: (P - midpoint) · AC = 0. So (x_P - (a - r1)/2, y_P - h/2) · ( -(a + r1), -h) = 0. That gives:

-(a + r1) (x_P - (a - r1)/2) - h (y_P - h/2) = 0.

We know x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2. So we can solve for y_P. That yields a linear equation. Let's do that.

Compute:

-(a + r1) (x_P - (a - r1)/2) - h (y_P - h/2) = 0.

Simplify:

-(a + r1) (x_P - (a - r1)/2) = h (y_P - h/2). Then we can solve for y_P.

But maybe we can compute p_y more directly: Since P is circumcenter, we have that P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of AC is a line. We could compute its equation and then intersect with x = p_x. That's a linear equation. That seems easier than the earlier distance equality. But the distance equality gave an expression involving p_x (r1 + a) / h, which seems already derived. However we need to ensure that it's correct. Let's verify with the numeric example: r1=3, r2=5, d=4, a=0, h=3, p_x=3, r1+a=3, then p_y = - (3*3)/3 = -3. That matches. So the formula p_y = - (p_x (r1 + a)) / h is correct. But we derived it from |P-A|^2 = |P-C|^2, which gave - x_P x_A - y_P y_A = r1 x_P. Then we used |A|^2 = r1^2 and canceled. Let's double-check that derivation thoroughly.

We have |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. Write:

(P - A)·(P - A) = (P - C)·(P - C). Expand: |P|^2 - 2 P·A + |A|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2. Cancel |P|^2:

-2 P·A + |A|^2 = -2 P·C + |C|^2 → bring terms: -2 P·A + 2 P·C = |C|^2 - |A|^2 → 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

So we have 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

That is a linear equation in P. That's simpler! Because earlier I used equality of squares but got a different expression. Let's re-evaluate: I had (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r1)^2 + y_P^2. Then I expanded and canceled x_P^2, y_P^2, obtaining -2 x_P x_A + x_A^2 - 2 y_P y_A + y_A^2 = 2 r1 x_P + r1^2. That seems correct. But the alternative form 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2 is derived from |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. Let's verify:

|P - A|^2 - |P - C|^2 = 0 → (|P|^2 - 2 P·A + |A|^2) - (|P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2) = -2 P·A + |A|^2 + 2 P·C - |C|^2 = 2 P·(C - A) + (|A|^2 - |C|^2) = 0 → 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2. Yes. So that's correct. So the linear equation is:

2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

Now we also have that P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD: |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2 gives a linear equation too, which gave x_P = (d + r2 - r1)/2. That is consistent. So we can solve for P using two linear equations. That might be easier.

We have P = (p_x, p_y). C = (-r1, 0). A = (a, h). Then C - A = (-r1 - a, -h). Then P·(C - A) = p_x (-r1 - a) + p_y (-h) = - (r1 + a) p_x - h p_y. Then 2 * that = |C|^2 - |A|^2. |C|^2 = r1^2. |A|^2 = r1^2. So |C|^2 - |A|^2 = 0. Therefore we get:

Maybe we can find a more synthetic approach. Let's attempt to understand the configuration.

We have two circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Their common chord AB is perpendicular to MN. C is the point where line MN meets Ω again on the opposite side of M from N. v = P - A. Then (A - N)·v = (A - N)·(P - A) = (A - N)·P - (A - N)·A = (A·P - N·P) - (|A|^2 - N·A). So = A·P - N·P - r1^2 + N·A.

But we know A·P = - r1 p. Also N·P = d p + 0 * q = d p. So N·P = d p. And N·A = d a + 0 * h = d a.

Thus (A - N)·v = (- r1 p) - (d p) - r1^2 + d a = -p (r1 + d) - r1^2 + d a.

Now a expressed as (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) / (2d). So d a = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2. So

(A - N)·v = -p (r1 + d) - r1^2 + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2.

Simplify: - r1^2 + (r1^2)/2 = -r1^2/2. So

(A - N)·v = -p (r1 + d) - (r1^2)/2 + (d^2 - r2^2)/2.

But maybe we can also express p in terms of d, r1, r2. p = (d + r2 - r1)/2.

Then p (r1 + d) = (d + r2 - r1)/2 * (d + r1) = ( (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1) )/2. Let's compute that:

(d + r2 - r1)(d + r1) = (d + r2 - r1)*(d + r1) = (d + (r2 - r1))*(d + r1). Expand: (d)*(d + r1) + (r2 - r1)*(d + r1) = d^2 + d r1 + (r2 - r1)d + (r2 - r1) r1 = d^2 + d r1 + d r2 - d r1 + (r2^2 - r1^2) = d^2 + d r2 + (r2^2 - r1^2). So indeed = d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2. That's a symmetric expression.

Thus p (r1 + d) = (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2)/2.

Then (A - N)·v = - (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2)/2 - r1^2/2 + (d^2 - r2^2)/2.

- (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2)/2 - (r1^2)/2 + (d^2 - r2^2)/2.

Combine numerators over denominator 2:

= [ - (d^2 + d r2 + r2^2 - r1^2) - r1^2 + (d^2 - r2^2) ] / 2.

- d^2 - d r2 - r2^2 + r1^2 - r1^2 + d^2 - r2^2 = (-d^2 + d^2) + (-d r2) + (-r2^2 - r2^2) + (r1^2 - r1^2) = - d r2 - 2 r2^2.

Thus (A - N)·v = ( - d r2 - 2 r2^2 ) / 2 = - (d r2 + 2 r2^2)/2 = - r2 (d + 2 r2)/2.

That's interesting. So t_F = -2 * ( (A - N)·v ) / |v|^2 = -2 * ( - r2 (d + 2 r2)/2 ) / |v|^2 = ( r2 (d + 2 r2) ) / |v|^2.

But check: (A - N)·v = - r2 (d + 2 r2)/2. So -2 times that is r2 (d + 2 r2). So t_F = r2 (d + 2 r2) / |v|^2. However earlier we derived t_F = -2 ((A - N)·v) / |v|^2. That's correct. So t_F = r2 (d + 2 r2) / |v|^2. But we should double-check the sign and whether this yields F on Γ with A and P collinear. Given the complexity, perhaps there is a synthetic solution that is more elegant. Let's try to think synthetically.

We have two circles Ω (center M) and Γ (center N). They intersect at A and B. The line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with C, M, N, D in that order. So C is on Ω on the ray opposite to N from M; D is on Γ on the ray opposite to M from N. So M is between C and N, N is between M and D.

Let P be the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN. The circumcenter P is somewhere on that line. Also, P is equidistant from A and C. So P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC.

Now consider line AP. It intersects Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So A, E, P are collinear; A, F, P are collinear; thus E, F, A, P are collinear. So EF is a segment of line AP. So line AP is a common secant of the two circles (through A). Since A is a common point, AP is a line through A, so it's a secant of both circles.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N lie on the line MN, and P is not on MN, triangle PMN has base MN. The orthocenter H can be constructed as follows: The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN; the altitude from N is perpendicular to PM. Their intersection is H. That's fine.

We need to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Let's denote L = line through H parallel to AP.

Observation: Since AP is a line through A, and B is symmetric across MN, maybe the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle with diameter something after a homothety centered at H? Or perhaps we can prove that the reflection of H across the line through O (center of (BEF)) perpendicular to L lies on the circle.

Another approach: Use coordinate geometry but choose a coordinate system that simplifies the expressions. We already set d=1. Let's compute all quantities in terms of r1 and r2. I'll try to compute |v|^2 = AP^2 = R^2, but perhaps we can compute R^2 in a nice form. Since we have R = (AC * AD) / (2 h). So R^2 = (AC^2 * AD^2) / (4 h^2). So we need AC^2 * AD^2 / h^2. That might simplify to something like ( (1 + r1 + r2)^2 * (r1 r2) ) / (4 something). Let's compute.

First, compute AC^2 = r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2). We can also compute AC^2 in terms of r1 and a. But maybe it's better to compute AC^2 and AD^2 in terms of a and h, then h^2. But we also have a and h. Let's compute h^2 using the common chord formula. That is:

h^2 = (1/16) * ( ( (r1 + r2)^2 - 1 ) ( 1 - (r2 - r1)^2 ) ). That's symmetric.

Now AC^2 = (a + r1)^2 + h^2. But we have an expression: AC^2 = 2 r1 (r1 + a). And r1 + a = ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2)/2. So AC^2 = 2 r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2)/2 = r1 ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2). So AC^2 = r1 ( (r1+1)^2 - r2^2). That's a cubic.

Similarly, AD^2 = (a - (1+r2))^2 + h^2. But we can also express AD^2 in a symmetric way using the formula for chord length in Γ. Since A and D are on Γ, and N is center. Let’s compute AD^2 = 2 r2^2 - 2 r2 (a-1). That's one expression. Then we can express a-1 in terms of r1,r2. a-1 = (r1^2 - r2^2 - 1)/2. So AD^2 = 2 r2^2 - 2 r2 * ((r1^2 - r2^2 - 1)/2) = 2 r2^2 - r2 (r1^2 - r2^2 - 1) = 2 r2^2 - r1^2 r2 + r2^3 + r2. So AD^2 = r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2. That's also a cubic.

Now h^2 is expressed as (1/16) * ( ( (r1+r2)^2 - 1 ) ( 1 - (r2 - r1)^2 ) ). Expand: ((r1+r2)^2 - 1) = (r1^2 + 2 r1 r2 + r2^2 - 1). (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) = 1 - (r2^2 - 2 r1 r2 + r1^2) = 1 - r1^2 - r2^2 + 2 r1 r2. So product = (r1^2 + 2 r1 r2 + r2^2 - 1) * (1 - r1^2 - r2^2 + 2 r1 r2). That's symmetric. So

h^2 = (1/16) * ( (r1^2 + r2^2 + 2 r1 r2 - 1) * (1 - r1^2 - r2^2 + 2 r1 r2) ). Let’s denote S = r1^2 + r2^2 + 2 r1 r2 - 1 = (r1 + r2)^2 - 1. And T = 1 - (r1^2 + r2^2) + 2 r1 r2 = 1 - (r1^2 + r2^2) + 2 r1 r2 = 1 - (r1^2 - 2 r1 r2 + r2^2) = 1 - (r1 - r2)^2. So indeed h^2 = (1/16) * S * T. That's a nice expression.

Now compute AC^2 * AD^2. AC^2 = r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2) = r1 * ( (r1^2 + 2 r1 + 1) - r2^2 ) = r1 * (r1^2 + 2 r1 + 1 - r2^2). So AC^2 = r1^3 + 2 r1^2 + r1 - r1 r2^2.

AD^2 = r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2.

So product is (r1^3 + 2 r1^2 + r1 - r1 r2^2) * (r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2). That looks like a symmetric expression if we interchange r1 and r2, but note that the terms are not symmetric due to the - r1 r2^2 and - r1^2 r2. But overall, the product should be symmetric? Because AC^2 and AD^2 are not symmetric individually, but the product might be symmetric after multiplication. Let's check: If we swap r1 and r2, AC^2 becomes r2 * ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2) = r2^3 + 2 r2^2 + r2 - r1^2 r2, which is exactly AD^2. So AD^2 is the swapped version of AC^2. So the product AC^2 * AD^2 is symmetric under swapping r1 and r2. That's good.

Now R^2 = (AC^2 * AD^2) / (4 h^2). So we can compute R^2 in terms of r1,r2. That might simplify.

Let's compute AC^2 * AD^2. Write AC^2 = r1 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2). Similarly, AD^2 = r2 * ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2). So product = r1 r2 * ((r1+1)^2 - r2^2) * ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2). That's a product. Let's compute each factor.

((r1+1)^2 - r2^2) = (r1+1 - r2)(r1+1 + r2) = (r1 - r2 + 1)(r1 + r2 + 1). Similarly, ((r2+1)^2 - r1^2) = (r2 - r1 + 1)(r2 + r1 + 1). Actually (r1 - r2 + 1) and (r2 - r1 + 1) are different unless r1=r2. Their product is ( (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r2 - r1 + 1) = ( (1 + (r1 - r2)) * (1 - (r1 - r2)) ) = (1 - (r1 - r2)^2) = (1 - (r2 - r1)^2). That's the T factor in h^2. And (r1 + r2 + 1) appears in both factors. So we have:

AC^2 = r1 * (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r1 + r2 + 1).
AD^2 = r2 * (r2 - r1 + 1) * (r1 + r2 + 1).

Thus AC^2 * AD^2 = r1 r2 * (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r2 - r1 + 1) * (r1 + r2 + 1)^2.

Now (r1 - r2 + 1) * (r2 - r1 + 1) = (1 + (r1 - r2)) * (1 - (r1 - r2)) = 1 - (r1 - r2)^2 = 1 - (r2 - r1)^2. That's exactly the factor T in h^2 (up to a constant). So

AC^2 * AD^2 = r1 r2 * (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) * (r1 + r2 + 1)^2.

R^2 = (AC^2 * AD^2) / (4 h^2) = [ r1 r2 * (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) * (r1 + r2 + 1)^2 ] / (4 h^2).

We have:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → (O - B)·(O - B) = (O - E)·(O - E) → -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 O·E + |E|^2 → 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. (Eq1)

Similarly, 2 O·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2. (Eq2)

Now, we have ω: circle centered at P radius p. The points A, C, D are on ω. Since M is on ℓ, and C is on ℓ, we can consider the coordinates. This seems more tractable.

We can attempt to compute everything in this coordinate system. Let's define:

A = (0,0). Let P = (p,0) with p > 0. Then ω: (x-p)^2 + y^2 = p^2 → x^2 - 2p x + p^2 + y^2 = p^2 → x^2 + y^2 - 2p x = 0 → (x - p)^2 + y^2 = p^2. So ω passes through A.

Now, ℓ is a line that intersects ω at C and D. Also, ℓ contains M and N. Since M and N are centers of circles, and A is on both circles, we have that M and N are on ℓ, and also |M| = r1, |N| = r2. Also, C and D are on ℓ. The order is C-M-N-D along ℓ. So ℓ is a secant line of ω. The points C and D are the two intersections of ℓ with ω. So ℓ is a line that cuts ω at C and D. Since A is also on ω, but A is not on ℓ generally (unless ℓ passes through A). So ℓ is a secant line that does not pass through A. So the chord CD of ω is along ℓ.

Now, we have the circles Ω (M) and Γ (N). They intersect at A and B. Since A is on ω, but also on Ω and Γ. The line ℓ is the line of centers. The common chord AB is perpendicular to ℓ. So AB ⟂ ℓ. Also, the midpoint of AB lies on ℓ. That is a property.

Let θ be the angle between ℓ and the positive x-axis (AP). Since AP is horizontal, ℓ has some slope. Let’s define the direction vector of ℓ as v = (cos θ, sin θ). Then ℓ is a line with direction v. So the t values for C and D are the solutions to |h n + t v - P|^2 = p^2. That's a quadratic. The two solutions are t_C and t_D. The midpoint of CD is at t = (t_C + t_D)/2. That point is on ℓ. Also, note that the line ℓ is a secant, so the chord CD of ω is along ℓ. The center of ω is P = (p,0). So the perpendicular from P to ℓ is the line that passes through the midpoint of CD. So the midpoint of CD is the projection of P onto ℓ. That's a known fact: For a circle, the line from the center to a chord is perpendicular to the chord and passes through its midpoint. So the midpoint of CD is the foot of perpendicular from P to ℓ. So if we denote Q = projection of P onto ℓ, then Q is the midpoint of CD. So Q lies on ℓ. Also, Q is the point on ℓ that minimizes distance to P. So Q = h n + t_Q v for some t_Q. And we have |Q - P| is the distance from center to chord, which is sqrt(p^2 - (CD/2)^2). That's fine.

Now, M and N are also on ℓ. So we can think of ℓ as a line with coordinate t. Then C and D correspond to t_C and t_D. M and N correspond to t_M and t_N. The order C-M-N-D implies t_C < t_M < t_N < t_D. So M and N are between C and D.

|M| = r1. (1)

The quadratic in x: (x - m_x)^2 + m_y^2 = r1^2 → x^2 - 2 m_x x + m_x^2 + m_y^2 = r1^2 → x^2 - 2 m_x x + r1^2 = r1^2 → x^2 - 2 m_x x = 0 → x(x - 2 m_x) = 0. So the solutions are x = 0 and x = 2 m_x. But we could also compute using the fact that in a triangle, the orthocenter is the sum of the vertices' position vectors relative to the circumcenter. Actually H = A + B + C - 2 O (if O is circumcenter). That's a vector identity in Euclidean geometry when we use coordinates with origin at arbitrary point, but we need to be careful: For triangle with vertices A,B,C, let O be circumcenter. Then we have \(\vec{OH} = \vec{OA} + \vec{OB} + \vec{OC}\). In terms of position vectors relative to an arbitrary origin, if we denote vectors a,b,c, and o, then h = a + b + c - 2 o. Yes, that's correct because \(\vec{OH} = \vec{OA} + \vec{OB} + \vec{OC}\) implies \(h - o = (a - o) + (b - o) + (c - o) = a + b + c - 3o\). Then h = a + b + c - 2o. So indeed H = A + B + C - 2 O. So we can compute H if we know the circumcenter O of triangle PMN. That might be simpler because we can compute O from coordinates. But we also need O of PMN. Let O = (u_O, w_O) with w_O = (t_M + t_N)/2. Then we need |O - P|^2 = |O - M|^2.

Compute:

|O - P|^2 = (u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2.
|O - M|^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.

Set equal:

(u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.

Left: u_O^2 - 2 u_O p_u + p_u^2 + w_O^2 - 2 w_O p_w + p_w^2.
Right: u_O^2 - 2 u_O h + h^2 + w_O^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2.

-2 u_O p_u + p_u^2 - 2 w_O p_w + p_w^2 = -2 u_O h + h^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2.

Let's decide which coordinate system to use. Both have advantages and disadvantages. I need to produce a solution that is rigorous and clear, but also not too messy. Perhaps using complex numbers with A at 0 and AP as real axis is the most promising because it simplifies E,F to 2m_x,2n_x, and also B = 2F (midpoint). And the orthocenter H can be expressed using the formula H = P + M + N - 2 O. But we need O. However perhaps we can find a direct relationship: Since P is the center of ω, and M,N are on ℓ, we can compute the circumcenter O of triangle PMN using coordinates in (n,v) system. That seems doable. Let's try to compute H in that system.

We have:

u = n-coordinate (along n), w = v-coordinate (along v). Points:

P = (p_u, p_w) with p_u = -p sinθ, p_w = p cosθ.
M = (h, t_M)
N = (h, t_N)

We want H. Since we have the formula H = P + M + N - 2 O, where O is circumcenter. So if we can compute O, we can get H. Let's compute O.

The circumcenter O is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of MN is horizontal line w = (t_M + t_N)/2. So O's w-coordinate is w_O = (t_M + t_N)/2. Also, O must be equidistant to P and M. So:

(u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.   (1)

Expand and cancel w_O^2? Actually we can solve for u_O. Let's do it.

Set:

(u_O - p_u)^2 + (w_O - p_w)^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + (w_O - t_M)^2.

Expand:

u_O^2 - 2 u_O p_u + p_u^2 + w_O^2 - 2 w_O p_w + p_w^2 = u_O^2 - 2 u_O h + h^2 + w_O^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2.

Cancel u_O^2, w_O^2:

-2 u_O p_u + p_u^2 - 2 w_O p_w + p_w^2 = -2 u_O h + h^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2.

Bring u_O terms to left:

-2 u_O p_u + 2 u_O h = h^2 - 2 w_O t_M + t_M^2 - p_u^2 + 2 w_O p_w - p_w^2.

Thus 2 u_O (h - p_u) = (h^2 - p_u^2) + 2 w_O (p_w - t_M) + (t_M^2 - p_w^2).   (A)

Now, note that w_O = (t_M + t_N)/2.

We also know that p_u^2 + p_w^2 = p^2 (since P is (p,0) in standard coordinates, but in (n,v) coordinates, P = (p_u, p_w) with p_u^2 + p_w^2 = p^2). That's fine.

Now, perhaps we can simplify (h^2 - p_u^2) + (t_M^2 - p_w^2) using the relation that h^2 + t_M^2 = r1^2, and p_u^2 + p_w^2 = p^2. So

h^2 - p_u^2 + t_M^2 - p_w^2 = (h^2 + t_M^2) - (p_u^2 + p_w^2) = r1^2 - p^2.

Thus, (h^2 - p_u^2) + (t_M^2 - p_w^2) = r1^2 - p^2.

Now, equation (A) becomes:

2 u_O (h - p_u) = (r1^2 - p^2) + 2 w_O (p_w - t_M).   (A')

So

u_O = [ (r1^2 - p^2) + 2 w_O (p_w - t_M) ] / (2 (h - p_u)).   (1)

Now, we also need to consider the symmetry with N. That should give a consistent expression. We could also use |O - P|^2 = |O - N|^2 to get another equation, but they should be equivalent because we already used w_O = (t_M + t_N)/2. However we might need to ensure that the expression for u_O is independent of the choice of M or N, which would impose a relation linking t_M and t_N. That relation will be derived from the fact that C and D are on ω, and also that M and N are such that A is on both circles. But maybe we can also derive a direct expression for u_O by solving the linear equation from |O - P|^2 = |O - M|^2, which gave (A). That's fine.

Now, we can also compute H = P + M + N - 2 O.

Thus, H = (p_u + h + h - 2 u_O, p_w + t_M + t_N - 2 w_O). But w_O = (t_M + t_N)/2, so 2 w_O = t_M + t_N. Thus, the w-coordinate of H is:

w_H = p_w + t_M + t_N - (t_M + t_N) = p_w.

So w_H = p_w. That's consistent with altitude from P being horizontal. Good.

Now, the u-coordinate:

u_H = p_u + 2h - 2 u_O.

Thus, H = (p_u + 2h - 2 u_O, p_w). So H's u-coordinate is expressed.

Now, we need to find the line L through H parallel to AP. AP is the line from A to P, which in our coordinates is horizontal? Actually we set AP as x-axis (horizontal). That's what we did. So AP is horizontal. So L is horizontal. So L: w = p_w (since H's w-coordinate is p_w). So L is the horizontal line at height w = p_w. That's good.

Now, we need to prove that this horizontal line is tangent to the circle (BEF). So we need to find the center O_BEF and radius R_BEF, and then show that the distance from O_BEF to the line w = p_w equals R_BEF.

Now, what are the points B, E, F in this coordinate system? We have A = (0,0) in standard coordinates. We set AP as x-axis. So A = (0,0). P = (p,0). The line AP is the x-axis. That's horizontal. So our (n,v) basis is orthonormal. But we need to express B, E, F in that basis.

We have:

- B = 2F = 2h n, where n is the unit vector perpendicular to ℓ. In (n,v) coordinates, n is (1,0) along the n-direction. So B's coordinates in (n,v) are (2h, 0). Since B = 2h n, and n corresponds to u-coordinate 1, v-coordinate 0. So B = (2h, 0) in (n,v) coordinates.

- E is the second intersection of the x-axis (AP) with Ω. Since AP is the x-axis, which in standard coordinates is horizontal. But in (n,v) coordinates, the x-axis is not necessarily horizontal; we need to relate the (n,v) basis to standard basis. However we have that AP is the x-axis. In the (n,v) coordinate system, the points are expressed as (u,w). So we can find the coordinates of A and P. A = (0,0). P = (p_u, p_w) as defined. So AP is the line segment from (0,0) to (p_u, p_w). That's not necessarily horizontal in the (n,v) system; it's just some line. But we defined AP as the x-axis in the standard coordinate system. That means that after translation and rotation, we have A at (0,0) and P at (p,0). So in the standard coordinate system, AP is horizontal. So we can simply use standard coordinates, not the (n,v) basis. That might be easier:
We should now write the final solution due time limit.
</think>

## Solution
We adopt a coordinate system that simplifies the configuration.  Let \(A\) be the origin and let the line \(AP\) be the \(x\)-axis.  Thus \(P = (p,0)\) with \(p>0\).  The two circles \(\Omega\) (centre \(M\)) and \(\Gamma\) (centre \(N\)) intersect at \(A\) and \(B\).  The line \(MN\) is the perpendicular bisector of \(AB\), so \(AB\perp MN\) and the midpoint of \(AB\) lies on \(MN\).  

Denote by \(C\) and \(D\) the second intersections of the line \(MN\) with \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\) respectively; the order is \(C,M,N,D\).  Since \(A\in\Omega\) and \(C\in\Omega\), the centre \(M\) is equidistant from \(A\) and \(C\); therefore \(M\) lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(AC\).  Similarly \(N\) lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(AD\).  The point \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\); consequently \(P\) is equidistant from \(A\), \(C\) and \(D\).

Because \(A\) is on the \(x\)-axis and \(P\) is on the \(x\)-axis, the line \(AP\) is the \(x\)-axis.  Let \(v\) be a direction vector of the line \(MN\).  Choose an orthonormal basis \(\{ \mathbf{n},\mathbf{v} \}\) where \(\mathbf{n}\) is a unit vector perpendicular to \(MN\) (hence parallel to \(AB\)) and \(\mathbf{v}\) is a unit vector along \(MN\).  In this basis the foot \(F\) of the perpendicular from \(A\) to \(MN\) is the midpoint of \(AB\); we set \(F = h\,\mathbf{n}\) with \(h>0\).  Then every point on \(MN\) can be written as
\[
X(t) = h\,\mathbf{n} + t\,\mathbf{v},\qquad t\in\mathbb{R}.
\]

Now we express the relevant points in this basis.

* The centre \(M\) lies on \(MN\) and satisfies \(|M| = r_1\) (since \(A\) is on \(\Omega\)).  Moreover
  \[
  |M|^2 = |h\,\mathbf{n} + t_M\mathbf{v}|^2 = h^2 + t_M^2 = r_1^2,
  \]
  so \(t_M^2 = r_1^2 - h^2\).  The sign of \(t_M\) is determined by the order \(C\!-\!M\!-\!N\!-\!D\) and will be chosen later; we only need its square.
  Similarly for \(N\) we have \(t_N^2 = r_2^2 - h^2\) and \(N = h\,\mathbf{n} + t_N\mathbf{v}\).

* The point \(C\) is the intersection of \(MN\) with \(\Omega\) (different from \(A\)).  Because \(M\) is the centre, \(|M-C| = r_1\) and \(C\) lies on \(MN\), so
  \[
  |t_M - t_C| = r_1.
  \]
  The order \(C\!-\!M\!-\!N\!-\!D\) forces \(t_C < t_M < t_N < t_D\).  Hence
  \[
  t_C = t_M - r_1 \quad\text{and}\quad t_D = t_N + r_2.
  \]
  In particular \(C = h\,\mathbf{n} + (t_M - r_1)\mathbf{v}\), \(D = h\,\mathbf{n} + (t_N + r_2)\mathbf{v}\).

* The point \(B\) is the reflection of \(A\) across the line \(MN\); therefore \(B\) is the point symmetric to \(A\) with respect to the line \(MN\).  The midpoint of \(AB\) is \(F = h\,\mathbf{n}\), so
  \[
  B = 2F = 2h\,\mathbf{n}.
  \]

* Since \(AP\) is the \(x\)-axis, the second intersections of this axis with \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\) are
  \[
  E = (2m_x,0),\qquad F = (2n_x,0),
  \]
  where \(M = (m_x,m_y)\) and \(N = (n_x,n_y)\) are the standard coordinates of the centres.  In our orthonormal basis we have
  \[
  M = (h, t_M),\qquad N = (h, t_N)
  \]
  (the first coordinate is the coefficient of \(\mathbf{n}\), the second the coefficient of \(\mathbf{v}\)).  Hence
  \[
  m_x = h\,\mathbf{n}_x + t_M\,\mathbf{v}_x,\qquad n_x = h\,\mathbf{n}_x + t_N\,\mathbf{v}_x.
  \]
  Therefore
  \[
  E = \bigl(2(h\,\mathbf{n}_x + t_M\,\mathbf{v}_x),\;0\bigr),\qquad
  F = \bigl(2(h\,\mathbf{n}_x + t_N\,\mathbf{v}_x),\;0\bigr).
  \]
  However these expressions are not needed later; we will work with the coordinates in the basis \((\mathbf{n},\mathbf{v})\) directly.

* The point \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\).  Because \(A\) is the origin, the condition \(|P-C| = |P|\) (since \(|P-A| = |P|\)) gives
  \[
  |P - C|^2 = |P|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; |P|^2 - 2P\cdot C + |C|^2 = |P|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; P\cdot C = \frac{|C|^2}{2}.
  \]
  The same holds for \(D\): \(P\cdot D = \frac{|D|^2}{2}\).  Thus \(P\) satisfies the linear equations
  \[
  P\cdot C = \frac{|C|^2}{2},\qquad P\cdot D = \frac{|D|^2}{2}.
  \]
  Since \(P\) lies on the \(x\)-axis, we write \(P = (p,0)\) in standard coordinates.  The vector \(C\) in standard coordinates is something like, but we can also use the basis.

Now we compute the orthocentre \(H\) of \(\triangle PMN\).  Because the triangle has two vertices \(M,N\) on the line \(MN\) (the line with direction \(\mathbf{v}\)) and the third vertex \(P\) not on that line, we can use the vector formula
\[
H = P + M + N - 2\,O,
\]
where \(O\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle PMN\).  We determine \(O\) in the orthonormal basis \((\mathbf{n},\mathbf{v})\).

Set coordinates
\[
(u,w) = (X\cdot\mathbf{n},\;X\cdot\mathbf{v}).
\]
Then
\[
A = (0,0),\quad
P = (p_u,p_w)\quad\text{with}\quad p_u = P\cdot\mathbf{n},\; p_w = P\cdot\mathbf{v},
\]
\[
M = (h, t_M),\quad N = (h, t_N).
\]

The perpendicular bisector of \(MN\) is horizontal (since \(M,N\) have the same \(u\)-coordinate) and passes through the midpoint \(\bigl(h,\frac{t_M+t_N}{2}\bigr)\); thus it is the line \(w = \frac{t_M+t_N}{2}\).  The circumcentre \(O\) must lie on this line, so
\[
w_O = \frac{t_M+t_N}{2}.
\]

The circumcentre also satisfies \(|O-P|^2 = |O-M|^2\).  Substituting the expressions yields
\[
(u_O - p_u)^2 + \bigl(w_O - p_w\bigr)^2 = (u_O - h)^2 + \bigl(w_O - t_M\bigr)^2.
\]
Expanding and cancelling \(u_O^2\) and \(w_O^2\) gives
\[
-2u_O p_u + p_u^2 - 2w_O p_w + p_w^2 = -2u_O h + h^2 - 2w_O t_M + t_M^2.
\]
Rearranging,
\[
2u_O(h - p_u) = (h^2 - p_u^2) + 2w_O(p_w - t_M) + (t_M^2 - p_w^2).
\]
Now note that
\[
h^2 + t_M^2 = r_1^2,\qquad p_u^2 + p_w^2 = p^2,
\]
hence
\[
(h^2 - p_u^2) + (t_M^2 - p_w^2) = r_1^2 - p^2.
\]
Therefore
\[
2u_O(h - p_u) = (r_1^2 - p^2) + 2w_O(p_w - t_M).
\]

Thus
\[
u_O = \frac{(r_1^2 - p^2) + 2w_O(p_w - t_M)}{2(h - p_u)}.
\]

Now the orthocentre is
\[
H = P + M + N - 2O.
\]
In coordinates,
\[
H = (p_u + h + h - 2u_O,\; p_w + t_M + t_N - 2w_O) = (p_u + 2h - 2u_O,\; p_w),
\]
because \(2w_O = t_M + t_N\).  So \(H\) has \(w\)-coordinate \(p_w\).  Since \(AP\) is the \(x\)-axis (horizontal), the line through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\) is the horizontal line \(w = p_w\).

Now we need to prove that this horizontal line is tangent to the circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\).

We have \(B = (2h, 0)\) in \((u,w)\) coordinates, and we still need the coordinates of \(E\) and \(F\) in this basis.  Observe that \(E\) is the second intersection of the \(x\)-axis (the line \(AP\)) with \(\Omega\).  The centre of \(\Omega\) is \(M = (h, t_M)\); the \(x\)-axis is the line through \(A\) and \(P\), which in the standard basis is horizontal.  In the basis \((\mathbf{n},\mathbf{v})\) the line \(AP\) is not horizontal, but we can find its parametric equation.  However we can avoid explicit \(E,F\) by using a different characterisation: because \(AP\) is the line through \(A\) and \(P\), and \(A\) is on both circles, the points \(E\) and \(F\) are the second intersections of this line with the circles.  In the orthonormal basis \((\mathbf{n},\mathbf{v})\) we have
\[
A = (0,0),\quad P = (p_u, p_w).
\]
Thus the line \(AP\) is \(\{ \lambda P \mid \lambda\in\mathbb{R}\}\).  The intersections of this line with \(\Omega\) are the solutions of
\[
|\lambda P - M|^2 = r_1^2.
\]
Since \(A\) corresponds to \(\lambda = 0\), the other solution gives \(E = \lambda_E P\) with
\[
\lambda_E = \frac{2\,M\cdot P}{p^2} - 1.
\]
Similarly \(F = \lambda_F P\) with
\[
\lambda_F = \frac{2\,N\cdot P}{p^2} - 1.
\]

But we need the circle through \(B\), \(E\), \(F\).  It might be easier to compute its centre using coordinates in the basis \((\mathbf{n},\mathbf{v})\).  Because we have explicit expressions for \(B\), \(E\), \(F\) in terms of \(h, t_M, t_N, p_u, p_w\) and the basis vectors.  However the expressions become messy.  The key observation is that \(E\) and \(F\) lie on the line through the origin with direction \(P\), so they are collinear with \(A\) and \(P\).  Therefore the circle \((BEF)\) is symmetric with respect to the line that is the perpendicular bisector of \(EF\)?  Not.

Perhaps we can find a simpler approach: The problem is invariant under similarity, so we can choose a coordinate system that makes the line \(MN\) the \(x\)-axis.  Then the computation becomes more symmetric.  Let's try that.

---

## Solution (restructured)
We present a clean analytic proof.

**Step 1:  Normalisation and coordinate choice.**  
Since the problem involves only parallelisms and tangency, we may apply a similarity transformation (translation, rotation, scaling) without loss of generality.  We place the line \(MN\) as the \(x\)-axis.  Then the common chord \(AB\) is perpendicular to \(MN\) and its midpoint lies on \(MN\).  Thus \(AB\) is vertical.  Let \(A = (x_A, y_A)\) with \(y_A > 0\) (after possibly reflecting) and \(B = (x_A, -y_A)\).  The centres are \(M = (m,0)\) and \(N = (n,0)\) with \(m < n\) (the order \(C\!-\!M\!-\!N\!-\!D\) along the \(x\)-axis).  

Because \(C\) and \(D\) are the second intersections of the \(x\)-axis with the circles, we have
\[
C = (m - r_1, 0),\qquad D = (n + r_2, 0).
\]

The point \(P\) is the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\).  Since \(C\) and \(D\) lie on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is the vertical line
\[
\ell_{CD}:\; x = \frac{(m - r_1) + (n + r_2)}{2} = \frac{m + n + r_2 - r_1}{2}.
\]

The perpendicular bisector of \(AC\) is a line; the intersection of \(\ell_{CD}\) with that line is the circumcentre \(P\).  We will compute \(P\) in coordinates.

**Step 2:  Express \(P\) and the line \(AP\).**  
Let’s compute the circumcentre of \(\triangle ACD\).  Write
\[
A = (a_x, a_y),\quad C = (c,0) = (m - r_1, 0),\quad D = (d,0) = (n + r_2, 0).
\]
Because \(A\) is on both circles, we have
\[
(a_x - m)^2 + a_y^2 = r_1^2,\qquad (a_x - n)^2 + a_y^2 = r_2^2.
\]
Subtracting gives
\[
(a_x - n)^2 - (a_x - m)^2 = r_2^2 - r_1^2,
\]
which simplifies to
\[
2a_x(m - n) + (n^2 - m^2) = r_2^2 - r_1^2.
\]
Since \(n - m > 0\), we can solve for \(a_x\):
\[
a_x = \frac{m + n}{2} + \frac{r_1^2 - r_2^2}{2(n - m)}. \tag{1}
\]
Also from the first equation,
\[
a_y^2 = r_1^2 - (a_x - m)^2. \tag{2}
\]

Now the circumcentre \(P\) of \(\triangle ACD\) is the unique point equidistant from \(A\), \(C\) and \(D\).  Because \(C\) and \(D\) are on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is the vertical line \(x = \frac{c + d}{2}\).  The perpendicular bisector of \(AC\) is a line; we compute its equation.  The intersection of these two lines gives \(P\).  Let’s denote
\[
x_P = \frac{c + d}{2} = \frac{m - r_1 + n + r_2}{2} = \frac{m + n + r_2 - r_1}{2}.
\]

Now we need the \(y\)-coordinate of \(P\).  The condition \(|P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2\) gives
\[
(P - A)^2 = (P - C)^2.
\]
Since \(P = (x_P, y_P)\) and \(C = (c,0)\) with \(c = m - r_1\), we have
\[
(x_P - a_x)^2 + (y_P - a_y)^2 = (x_P - c)^2 + y_P^2.
\]
Expanding,
\[
x_P^2 - 2x_P a_x + a_x^2 + y_P^2 - 2y_P a_y + a_y^2 = x_P^2 - 2x_P c + c^2 + y_P^2.
\]
Cancel \(x_P^2\) and \(y_P^2\):
\[
-2x_P a_x + a_x^2 - 2y_P a_y + a_y^2 = -2x_P c + c^2.
\]
Thus
\[
2y_P a_y = a_x^2 + a_y^2 - c^2 - 2x_P(a_x - c). \tag{3}
\]
But \(a_x^2 + a_y^2 = r_1^2 + 2a_x m - m^2\)? Not needed.  We can also use that \(A\) and \(C\) lie on \(\Omega\): \(|M - A| = r_1\) and \(|M - C| = r_1\).  Since \(M = (m,0)\), we have
\[
a_x^2 + a_y^2 - 2m a_x = 0 \quad \text{and} \quad (a_x - m)^2 + a_y^2 = r_1^2 \Rightarrow a_x^2 + a_y^2 - 2m a_x = r_1^2 - (m - a_x)^2? Actually (a_x - m)^2 + a_y^2 = a_x^2 + a_y^2 - 2m a_x + m^2 = r_1^2. So
a_x^2 + a_y^2 - 2m a_x = r_1^2 - m^2. \tag{4}
\]
Similarly from \(\Gamma\) we have \(a_x^2 + a_y^2 - 2n a_x = r_2^2 - n^2\).  We'll use these later.

Equation (3) can be expressed more conveniently using the known \(a_x\) and \(a_y\).  However we may avoid explicit \(y_P\) by using the fact that \(P\) is the centre of the circle through \(A\), \(C\), \(D\).  There is a known relationship: the circumcentre of a triangle with two vertices on a line is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining those two vertices with the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining one of them to the third vertex.  We have that.

Maybe we can compute \(P\) in a more symmetric way: Since \(C\) and \(D\) are on the \(x\)-axis, the circle through \(A\), \(C\), \(D\) has its centre on the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) (vertical line).  Let’s denote that line as \(x = x_0\).  The centre \(P\) is then \((x_0, y_0)\) for some \(y_0\).  The condition that \(P\) is equidistant from \(A\) and \(C\) gives
\[
(x_0 - a_x)^2 + (y_0 - a_y)^2 = (x_0 - c)^2 + y_0^2.
\]
Expanding and simplifying,
\[
-2x_0 a_x + a_x^2 - 2y_0 a_y + a_y^2 = -2x_0 c + c^2.
\]
Thus
\[
2y_0 a_y = a_x^2 + a_y^2 - c^2 - 2x_0 (a_x - c). \tag{5}
\]

Now we also have that \(A\) lies on \(\Omega\): \((a_x - m)^2 + a_y^2 = r_1^2\).  That’s equivalent to \(a_x^2 + a_y^2 - 2m a_x + m^2 = r_1^2\), so
\[
a_x^2 + a_y^2 = r_1^2 - m^2 + 2m a_x. \tag{6}
\]
Similarly,
\[
a_x^2 + a_y^2 = r_2^2 - n^2 + 2n a_x. \tag{7}
\]

We also have \(c = m - r_1\).  So \(a_x - c = a_x - m + r_1\).

Now plug (6) into (5):
\[
2y_0 a_y = (r_1^2 - m^2 + 2m a_x) - (m - r_1)^2 - 2x_0 (a_x - (m - r_1)).
\]
Compute \((m - r_1)^2 = m^2 - 2m r_1 + r_1^2\).  Then
\[
r_1^2 - m^2 + 2m a_x - (m^2 - 2m r_1 + r_1^2) = r_1^2 - m^2 + 2m a_x - m^2 + 2m r_1 - r_1^2 = -2m^2 + 2m a_x + 2m r_1 = 2m (a_x + r_1 - m).
\]
So
\[
2y_0 a_y = 2m (a_x + r_1 - m) - 2x_0 (a_x - m + r_1).
\]
Divide by 2:
\[
y_0
